BERLIN. Oct. 1Lâ Russia and Japan
act as though either would fight if the

as understood officially from reports re

able arrangement. Neither Government
has gone so far that It must fight or be
humiliated, but either Cabinet by a single

violently dfsturbed any day, though no ul
timatum has yet been thrown upon either
scale.

tional Bank, was brought back to Chicago

to having stolen $20,000 from tha .Institu

the power of the siren that he was Influ
enced to commit the crime and to blight
the life of his devoted wife.
George S. Forbes, whom Dunton had in
duced to become his accomplice, ended his
life by suicide when the crime was dis
covered. His death was indirectly the re
sult of the far-reaching influence of the
beautiful and daring adventuress. Forbes
was teller in the bank. Dunton would
draw personal checks, which Forbe3
would honor. Then Dunton would steal
the checks and the books would be tam
pered with. *

EASTON. Pa., Oct. 11.â The Delaware
River began to recede about- midnight
and continues to fall rapidly. The Le
hlgh and the Bushklll are still backed
up for miles, and everything along their
banks is flooded. It is . Impossible to es
timate the loss or even approximate, it,
but in Easton and towns up and down
the Delaware It will take millions to re
place the bridges and repair the damage.
Only one bridge from the headwaters of
the Delaware to Easton is standing, and
that is an old covered structure at Port
land.
The Jersey Central bridge is seriously
damaged, and at least three spans will
have to be replaced. The bridges at
Riegelsville, Mllford and Frenchtown
were washed away.
The Belvidere and Delaware division
of the Pennsylvania* Railroad is closed,
and trains are stalled here. Big washouts
occurred up and down the- road. The
North Delaware River highway is dam
aged to the extent of $20,000.
Mayor Lehr directed a relief corps, com
posed of policemen, National Guardsmen
and citizens, that rescued hundreds of
flood-bound residents along the Lehigh
River. The refugees were housed in the
City Guard armory.
The West Easton Industries are still
submerged; also the gas and electric light
and power plants. Zearfoss & Milliard.
lumber merchants suffered $50,000 dam
age. r . . . , r .

The Delaware and Raritan Canal and
the canal feeder both are reported to have
several breaks in their banks at different
points. Assistant Suprintendent Dunn of
the canal company said to-night that he
thought these breaks would be repaired â¬o
permit the resumption of traffic by Tues

About 100 houses in South Trenton are
still partially under water by reason of
the overflowing of the river. The city's
The railroad officials here have only
meager Information as to the condition
of the Belvidere division, as all the tele
graph wires are down with the exception
of a single one between this city and
Lambertville and that works imperfectly.
Railroad traffic on the Amboy division
from this city to Camden is suspended.
The tracks along the river between this
city and Bordentown are submerged at
points to a depth of three feet and trains
are not running. Traffic on the main line
of the Pennsylvania Railroad is now being
conducted with little deviation from the
regular schedule.
The two wagon bridges between this
city and Morrisville were both damaged
by the wreckage from the other bridges
coming down the river. The Calhoun
street bridge was so badly damaged that
to-day no traffic across it was allowed.
Many washouts are reported from differ
ent points, and In some places the tracks
are submerged by the overflowing of the
river.
TRENTON. N. J., Oct. 11.â The extent
of the damage done by the flood in this
section is now beginning to be fully
realized. At least six and possibly all
nine of the wagon and foot bridges across
the Delaware River between this city and
Easton, Pa., have been carried away.
. The one iron bridge between Trenton
and Easton is that crossing the river from
Point . Pleasant, Pa., to Byram, N. J.
This bridge, which is about half way be
tween Trenton and Easton, is believed to
be the only one not destroyed. These
bridges were erected at an average cost of
$50,000 each.

Enormous Damage Is Done in the
. Vicinity of Trenton.
The Pennsylvania and Lehigh Valley
railroads, except for minor interruptions,
practically resumed their regular sched
ules to-day. All Western mails arrived
here practically on schedule time.
The roads most affected by the storm,
the Erie and Lackawanna, were still
badly tied up on their main lines. On
their various branches It will take days
to repair the damage.
The damage to the Erie was the most
widespread. On its main line to-day
trains got as far as Passaic. On several
of the branches there was little or no
traffic. On the New Jersey and New
York division there were serious and fre
quent interruptions past Spring Valley.
The Lackawanna put up notices which
read: "All, tickets to Washington and
West .sold at passenger's risk."
During the day the mala line was cut
off service between Paterson and Boon
ton. Traffic ceased also on the Sussex
branch, the line being washed out In sev
eral places.

Passaic is a greater sufferer from the
flood than eve/i Paterson. Twenty mills
in Passaic alone are under water and 8000
workers are .out of employment. Ten
thousand persons have left their homes.
Eight hundred houses are flooded to the
eaves and in all 1000 acres are covered
by water from a depth of a few inches to
twenty feet. Several million feet of lum
ber have been carried down to Newark
Bay.
In Weaselbrook the Erie tracks are sus
pended in the air, the twenty-foot em
bankment having been washed away.
Twenty three-story frame houses have
gone -floating down the Passaic River and
as far as can be estimated the loss of
city property, real estate and buildings
is $2,000,000.
The maximum height of the flood was
two feet above the flood of 1902. To-night
it is receding.
Twenty-five bridges are down in Pas
saic and Sussex counties, on the Su'sque
hanna Railroad. Street Commissioner
Breet fears that many houses on River,
Main and West streets will fall.
Seven men are now in peril of their
lives in the Kearns Bros.' dye house on
the river margin near Paterson. A part
of the works has' been carried away. The
men have been In that part which re
mains standing since Friday noon.- While
they were trying to save thousands of
dollars' worth of silks by carrying them,
to the top of the building the structure;
was surrounded by water and the men
were cut off from all assistance because
of the torrent that has been rushing by
ever since. It is believed that tha entire
building will be carried from its founda
tion unless the flood subsides very soon.
The suffering in Wellington and Dut
tonvllle has been very great, as no re
lief has been able to reach these places.
All through this district houses and out
buildings, together with a great quantity
of dead stock, may be seen floating about.

SUFFERN. N. Y.. Oct. ll.-The storm
that prevailed through thla section for
the past two days was one of the most
disastrous ever known here. The trestle
on the Piedmont branch Is a total wreck.
Western trains are unable to proceed
beyond this place.
The town of Hlllburn. one mile from
here. Is cut off. as all the bridges have
been washed away. The town of Ramapo
Is a almost in ruins. Many persons are
homeless. The Iron works at Hlllburn
are flooded, and It Is feared that no work
will be done for a long time. Five hun
dred employes will be affected. Work ha*
been stopped at all the shops, and 1000
men are Idle.
Supplies . are short, as very few trains
from New York are bringing In freight.

SPOKANE. Wash.. Oct. 11.â ETmer Pe
trie of Sookane was shot to .leath by a
thug on a Northern Pacific freight train
a short distance west of the high Hans
man Creek trestle shortly after 3 o'clock;
to-night. Robbery was the motive of the
murder. G. E. Fu>Kerson, a harvester,
who lives at North Yaklma, was the only
eye witness cf the murder except the ac
complice of the man who fired the shot.
Fulkerson and the victim of th? murder
were traveling together In a lumber cap
from the harvest fields to Spokane, ac
cording to Fulkerson's story, when tho
two men came over the top of the train
and approached them. The four talked
a minute, and then one of the strangers
fired a shot over Fulkerson's h^ l ad. Pe
trle fell over against the lumber pile. Tha
two men then ordered FulkerÂ«on to hold
up 'his hands, searched him and made him
get off the swiftly moving train. Fulker
son landed safely, walked to Spokane and.
told the police his story. The train had
arrived, and In the lumber. Just whera
Fulkerson said the body would be found.
It wai found. The man was dead.
"I had been working as a harvest hand
in the southern part of the county." said
Fulkeraon. "and yesterday I wanted to
come to Spokane. I took a freight train
on the Ssokane and Palouse ac Plaza,
and rode in the caboose. At SD<insIe Pe
trie cot on. The car we were In was %
coal car loaded with lumber. We sat down
between the lumber pile and the end oC
the car. Pretty soon two men came from
toward th* back of the car and sat down
on the edge of the lumber pile on the car
right behind our car. They were sitting
where they cou!d look down on us. Sud
denly one of the men reached a gun right
over my head and fired at Petrle, who
was sitting behind met The shot blinded
me for a moment, and then one of tha
men grabbed me. while the othtr went
through my pockets and took my purse.
"When they told me to Jump off tha
train it was going pretty fast, and I
hated to jump. They told me to be quick
about it. and I Jumped off. As I got off
I heard one of them say: 'I guess we have
shot the other .' That was
the last I saw of thÂ«m. I could not de
scribe the men. Neither of them waa
masked. My Impression is that they
were hoboes. They were roughly dress
ed and wore dark clothes, as near as I
could make out."

cality was first discovered by the 16-year

prisoner who escaped. Is still at large, but
the prison officials look for his early
capture. In company with a man sup

miles up Emigration Canyon. Since then
no trace has been found of either man.
although many possea. spurred on by the

sant hunt thoughout the day.

Colonel Richard . Henry
Savage Passes Away â¢
in New York.
Special Disp&tch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Oct. VLâ Colonel Richard
Henry Savage, author and soldier, died
last night at the Roosevelt Hospital from
injuries received on the night of October
3, when he was run down at Sixth avenue
and Forty-second street by ~a delivery
wagon.. \-.-i '.Â¦'.';'>. 'vv;^ 1 :.^':^
Born In Utica, N. Y., in 1847, Richard
Henry Savage was destined to see many
lands and many phases of human life. He
was sent in 1S64 to me United States Mli
itary Academy, from which four years
later he was graduated. After serving a
few years as lieutenant in the army he re
tired to civil life until 1871, when he joined
the Egyptian forces, where he remained
for three years. Returning-, to this- co'un
try he entered the employ of the South
ern Railway, and remained with that cor
poration for ten years.
He was married to Miss Anna Josephine
Sheible, - a talented Russian . woman, In
1874, and It was through her Influence that
he turned his attention to literature! His
varied experiences furnished abundant
material. His most successful books were
"My^Offlcial Wife," | which has been dra
matized, and "Under the- Shadow of the
Pyramids." His contributions to * fiction*
have been numerous.
Colonel . Savage -volunteered- for service
in the Spanish-American war and was
senior, major of the Second United States
Volunteers. He - personally - directed the
raising of the American flag over the city
of Havana. ' . . '
Ocean City Practically Deserted.
.OCEAN CITY, .Md., Oct. ll.-Thls city
is ' practically deserted to-night. Owing
to the hurricane and high tides the great
er part of the city has been submerged
since Thursday night and many of the
residents have sought shelter on higher
ground. In addition to the damage re
ported in : last night's dispatcher, another
section of the front of : Congress Hall, a
leading hotel, about 100 feet in extent was
torn away to-day. The entire ocean front
of, the Windsor Hotel collapsed. A large
number of cottages , were I either washed
from their foundations or destroyed. {

MISS HENNEY'S DEATH.
Beals was born in Lafayette, Ind., and
was 31 years of age. About four years
ago he was employed at the Home for the
Adult Blind in Oakland and shortly after
leaving this place he. commenced making
balloon ascensions, being employed at dif
ferent places of amusement throughout
the State. His wife and mother have al
ways been opposed to his risking his life
as an aeronaut, and, although he often
promised them that he would abandon the
dangerous profession, like many others he
went up once too often.
He was hurt Sunday, September 27, while
making an ascension at "Coney Island."
but at that time his injuries were not seri
ous. When he went home to dinner yes
terday he told his wife that he was going
to the city, but did not tell her that he
was about to . make an ascension. She
knew nothing of his death until after 9
o'clock last evening.
Beals was well known In Oakland, where
he lived for sixteen years. He was a fire
man In the employ of. the Southern Pa
cific Company and lived at 812 Wood
street with his wife and 6-year-old daugh
ter.
His mother, Mrs. M. J. Beals, lives at
S02 Wood street, where she keeps a rail
road boarding-house. '

BURLINGTON, N. J., Oct. 11.â This

the place being under water caused by
the tremendous Â» flood in the Delaware
River. The highest point of the town is
only three feet above the flood In some

much of the surrounding country Is also
under water. In East Burlington It is es
timated that 90 per cent of the houses are

PHILADELPHIA. Oct. 11.â Delaware
avenue, which runs along the, Delaware
River, was again flooded for a distance of
two miles in this city at high tide this
afternoon. The water was so high that
ferry-boats were unable to enter their
slips without doing damage and for sev
eral hours service between this city and
Camdcn and Gloucester was suspended.
The cellars of most of the business houses
on Delaware avenue were flooded and
thousands of dollars' worth of goods were
either ruined or damaged. The water in
the avenue receded with the tide, but it
Is feared that the next high tide will send
the water up into the thoroughfare again,
as the river is still far above normal.

"W. A. Bradford of 1185 Market street is
perhaps the most accurate witness to the
tragedy. Bradford was driving with his
wife along, the road that encircles the
chain of lakes in the park when he saw
the aeronaut descending but a few feet in
front of him and within thirty feet of.
one of the lakes. Bradford drew up his
horse, and as he did so Beals. .who was
within forty feet of the ground, suddenly
released his, hold of the trapeze bar and
plunged to the roadway in front of the
horse. Tossing the lines to his: wife the
driver ran to the assistance * of the ' In
jured man, but realized that human help
was of no avail. The balloonist, his skull
fractured and his ' bones shattered, was
breathing his last.
Bradford, however, asked bis wife to
Beals, who was a fireman employed by
the Southern Pacific Company, was en
gaged by Charles W. Vosmer, manager
of the Coney Island Gardens, to make a
series of ascensions. He has been in the
business for about four years and has, at
different times, been under Vosmer's
management. He was considered expert
and reliable and was advertised as a star
feature of the new attraction at the
beach. When the vaudeville programme
had been rendered all was made ready for
the ascension. The massive balloon was
cut loose from its moorings and the aero
naut was carried toward the sky.
The ascension was one of the prettiest
ever seen in the city. There was scarce
ly any wind and tne balloon rose straight
upward to a distance of probably 1500
feet. There was some fear for a time
on the part of the crowd that witnessed
the ascension that the' bag would drift
over the sea and that the aeronaut would
be drowned. All fears were allayed,
however, when the parachute, cut loose,
floated Inland, though the balloon after
the gas had escaped fell on the beach,
within a few feet of the ocean.
Beals amused the throng of spectators
with all sorts of antics. He went up
clinging to the trapeze by his toes, and
when far above the e'arth could be seen
twisting about in gymnastics and ap
peared much at east at the dangerous
height. When he cut his parachute loose
and started back in the direction of terra
tirma he continued his tricks, and when
he was lost to view behind tho trees r.eo
ple expressed themselves as delighted at
what they thought was a successful as
cension. It was not until some time later
that the news of the accident reached the
pleasure grounds. The first intimation
that Vosmer had that all had not gone'
â¢well was when news came from the Park
Hospital that the body of his employe
had been taken there in a buggy. :
AERONAUT STRIKES GROUND.
BEALS CARRIED Â¦ UPWARD.
- f"^ EATH sped in the wake of a bal-
loon that cast its moorings at
JJ Coney Island Gardens yesterday
< afternoon. The aeronaut, Wll
. ' Ham H. Beals, who accompanied
the- inflated canvas bag to dizzy heights,
was dashed to the ground, mangled and
gasping his last, and a young woman,
Miss Madge Henney, who was watching
Its flight' against the sky, was thrown
from an electric car and died from her
injuries soon afterward.
William H. Beals, who now lies In the
Morgue, was an expert at his business.
He had been wafted toward the clouds
hundreds of times and such feats came to
be regarded by him as little more than
child's play, j As he hung in midair just
before the accident he performed feats
of daring on the bar of his parachute and
seemed to revel in the sport. It was not
until he was within a few feet of the
ground that he suddenly released his
hold, for some, unaccountable reason, and
pitched to the roadway beneath him. At
the same time Miss Madge Henney, a
pretty 17-year-old girl, residing at 1252
Folsom street, was being picked up with
a fractured skull and preparations were
being made to convey her to the Park
Hospital, where she expired. Her anx
iety to follow the flight of the unfortun
ate aeronaut brought her to a Bimllar
fate. While watching from a car her
head struck an electric pole, fracturing
her skull and throwing her to the side of
the track.

HONOLULU, Oct. 5.-Charles Walker of
Honolulu. has designed plans for a yacht
which he will offer to Sir Thomas Upton
and with which he hopes the Irish, tea
merchant will be able to lift the Amer
ica's cup next year . from the New York
Yacht Club. Walker is the designer of
many boats which have made a good
showing In Hawaiian waters. The new
boat is designed on entirely new and orig
inal lines, and Walker's, way of steering
and ballasting will be different from the
other boats LIpton has entered in the in
ternational yacht j races. - Walker was
born in Japan of English parents and has
never been naturalized, so that LIpton
could not object to the design on account
of his nationality.

signs a Yacht to Compete for
the America's Cup. : ' \

Â¦were riding suddenly became frightened

PARIS, Oct. 12.â The Figaro this morn
ing, publishing from . an "authoritative
source" the statement that the Czar's
visit to Rome has been postponed, adds:
"This decision will not affect the good
relations existing between the courts of
Rome and St. Petersburg. The mere raiÂ«
ing in certain political circles of the ques
tion whether the Czar would be welcomed
suffices to wound Russian susceptibilities.
Czar Nicholas is not afraid of hostile
demonstrations, but he cannot carry out
the plans for his visit while the Socialist
party is discussing its attitude toward a
sovereign who Is not merely visiting a
friendly court, but the whole Italian na
tion.

Royal Friend, H. L. Meharry's fast
greyhound, took first honors at the open
stake yesterday at Ingleside Coursing
Park, beating Cloverdale In the deciding
course by a score of 16 to 7. Cloverdale
ruled favorite and at rlip time was a 1 to
2 choice. / â¢
As the dogs went up the field the odds
looked justifiable, as the dog that the tal
<-nt had placed its money on was out at
the lead a good five lengths. As soon as
they got to work Royal Friend showed his
cleverness and in a mix-up outpointed his
opponent and gained an easy victory.
It was a good day for the talent, though
the occasional short ends came through
at fair odds. Toward the end of the stake
Jerry Wright looked like a winner, but in
a bye with Doreen was taken a route
that put him out of the running. The
dogs were sent after a jack that led them
a chase all over the field. Slipper Ed
Shostag caught the hare as it neared the
slip stand and threw it over the fence.
The hounds had gone a full three minutes
and even with the assistance of four re
lief dogs were forced to let the hare out
foot them. Jerry Wright was a 1 to 4 shot,
but when the flag went up he was out
pointed by the bye dog.

Doheny first showed signs of insanity
several weeks ago, when he suddenly de
serted the Pittsburg club In' an Ohio city
while laboring under the delusion that he
was being followed by detectives. After
a few weeks' rest at his home here he re
joined the team, but did not regain his
old form. When the season closed Do
heny was a nervous wreck and since
then has been constantly under the care
of a physician.
ANDOVER, Mass., Oct. 11.â After fell
ing his nurse with a terrific blow on the
head with a stove poker, Edward Do
heny. the Pittsburg National League
baseball pitcher, for more than an hour
to-day, armed with the same weapon, held
a score of neighbors and several police
men at bay. Finally he was overpowered
and after an examination by physicians
was adjudged Insane and committed to
the asylum at Danvers.
The nurse, Oberlin Howarth, is seri
ously injured, but it Is believed he will
recover.
Edward Doheny, the Pittshurg Na
tional League Pitcher, Is a
. - Nervous Wreck.

LONDON. Oct. 11.â The influx of small
Investors in consols and home rails last
week failed to neutralize the depressing
influences 1 which prevailed in the Ameri
can and Kaffir departments of the stock
market. The depression in the former
case was owing to the reduced steel cor
poration dividend and the revelations re
garding the United States Shipbuilding
Company, and In the second case to the
troubles at Amsterdam and the reports
that the Scotch banks were squeezing
borrowers. The small purchases In con
sols at the existing low prices were so
numerous as to make quite a record week
In the number of transfers, but in the
other markets buslne'sa was on the small
est scale and the prospects are far from
rt>sy, the Russo-Japanese war being add
ed to other disturbing features.
Business Is Transacted on a Small
Scale and the Prospects Are
Far From Rosy.

The sound steamboat City of Worcester
of the Norwich line, which was supposed
to have struck on the rocks near Execu
tion light late last night, simply an
chored, because a metal pin connected
with the walking beam had broken.
While at anchor temporary repairs were
made and the City of Worcester, under
her own steam, made her way to her
dock In the North River. Captain Row
land attempted to transfer his 175 pas
sengers to the New Hampshire of the
Stonington line, which came up a short
time after the accident, but as the waves
were too high the attempt was aban
doned. More than 150 of the passengers
were sent to New London by train and
those that stood by the broken down boat
proceeded on her to their destination to
night.
NEW YORK, Oct. 11.â The fierce storm
that has been raging along the North At
lantic coast for several days has seri
ously Interfered with shipping, and to
day only four of the ocean liners, all of
which had been greatly delayed, succeed
ed in making port. The four steamships
that arrived reported hurricanes and
mountainous seas. The Monterey of the
Ward line and the Jefferson of the Old
Dominion line came in from the south.
The latter craft was terribly battered by
the storm and, when boarded by a high
wave on Saturday, Richard Mcl^ane, the
lookout,. was seriously Injured, the Rev.
B. Hawley, a clergyman of North Caro
lina, was thrown across the cabin and
killed.

Returning to the subject of the Gov
ernment experimental station Senator
Dietrich remarked that he considered its
maintenance a waste of money and Its
further existence a folly. Continuing he
said:
What Alaska needs most are good wag-on
roads and more railroads. One should be built
between VaJde* and Eagle City on the Yukon
River, where it would penetrate a country rich
not only In minerals, but having a Just claim
to exceptional advantages in an agricultural
way. The commercial bodies on the entire
coast should take up this matter and encourage
some enterprising corporation to undertake the
construction* of the road, for it would be of
great benefit to the people in your State and
those north of California.

f United States Senator C. H. Dietrich of
Nebraska and fits daughter, who h&ve
been making an extended tour of Alaska,
arrived in port yesterday or! the United
States cutter Thetis and are registered
at the Palace Hotel. The. Senator and
Miss Dietrich left Seattle'last June for
Alaska, through which they have since
traveled many thousands of miles.
They spent about a month in and
around Dawson and, coming out by way
of the Yukon, they visited bÂ£ Michael
and other points of Interest. Returning
to the coast they boarded the Thetis,
which carried them up the coast to a
point near Point Barrow, which they were
prevented from reaching owing to the
ice. They also visited the DJamede Isl
ands and crossed the straits to the Sibe
rian coast, where the Thetis mado several
stops. In discussing his trip last even
ing the Senator said:
As a member of the Senate Land Committee
I discovered some months ago that we knew
very little about Alaska, and I concluded to
visit the country and see for myself Just what
it amounted to. A careful inspection of the ter
ritory has impressed me with its Importance
as a mineral country, but I have satisfied my
self that It will never be a successful agricul
tural district. /
The most striking scene of the burlesque
is the ''Storm" ballet in the second act.
Over 100 persons take part, ranging from
tiny children to plump womanhood. The
scene is Intended to symbolize, the pass
ing of a thunderstorm on a sunshiny day
and the idea is cleverly and elaborately
worked out with much beauty and grace.
Baby Jessamine, the smallest premier
danseuse in the world, appeared in this
scene and was heartily applauded.
The burlesque "Cleopatra" opened at
the Grand Opera-house at yesterday's
matinee and will hold the boards for the
remainder of the week- The dialogue is
worse than ordinary, but this is atoned
for by the scenery, music and ballets, all
of which are very beautiful.
The three Crane brothers present a
sketch which they call "The Mudtown
Minstrels." Nothing funnier has been
seen at the playhouse In many moons.
"Special announcement" will be a phrase
used all over town. The interlocutor In
troduces great singers and the music does
the rest. The nonsense these clever com
edians hand out would make a sick man
laugh himself into health. "Wallace
Brownlow's barytone voice must be heard
to be appreciated. In full and mellow
tones he rendered sweet melodies and
with dramatic power. He scored a hit.
The Rappo sisters, famous Russian
dancers, are very graceful and their ef
forts were highly appreciated. The Golden
Gate Quartet, assisted by Fanny Wlnfred,
has new songs and changes this week
and is as popular as before. Wood and
Ray present "The Old Jokes'. Home."
The sketch gives Ray a chance to show
his versatility as a comedian. The Pant
zer trio present "A Gymnast's Parlor En
tertainment." The work by the girls is
remarkable. The clown Is also very
funny. Clayton White and Marie Stew
art repeat the success they achieved last
week in the one-act comedy, "Paris."
Miss Stewart is a clever woman and her
imitations and general all-round acting
have won her many admirers In this city.
White also does some good work as
Max the Goat, a ward heeler. A. P. Ros
tow, the world's famous equilibrist, closes

Colomblno is the bright particular star
In a galaxy at the Orpheum this week.
He Is a marvelous change artist and kept
his audience In a state of wonder by the
rapidity In which he changed his cos
tumes In playing a sketch which called
for the Impersonation of nine characters.
This In itself is well worth seeing, but
he goes one better by caricaturing the

splendid orchestra furnishes the music
and he leads the men in selections that
have become familiar to all lovers of
music. His take-oft on Mascagni, Sousa
and CreatorÂ©, leaders who have been
seen In this city, were extremely funny
and created a great deal of merriment.
There are some catchy songs, which are
fairly well sung. The chorus is well
gowned and some clever dance steps are
thrown Into the general potpourl.

Hancock subsequently brought suit In
the Superior Court for the unpaid balance
of the year's salary, amounting to $600,
ar?d obtained Judgment. An appeal was
then taken by the city. Simultaneously
another suit was brought by Miss Mary
A. Polk, a teacher of the High School, on
the same grounds. This case was tried
with the same result and the decision of
the Supreme Court covers both cases.
The main point of the decision is that
contracts entered into with teachers by
one school board must be carried out by
succeeding boards and that salaries must
be paid during the life j of the contract
except In cases where service Is discon
tinued by mutual consent. The Hancock
and Polk cases have been before the
courts for the past -three years.
SANTA BARBARA. Oct. 11.â Word has
Just been received that the State Supreme
Court has decided the case of John O.
Hancock, formerly principal of the Santa
Barbara High School, against the City
Board of Education. The decision Is of
great Interest to all the school boards in
California. Hancock was dismissed by the
Board of Education, which succeeded th"e
High School Board, the latter being legis
lated out of existence. Hancock, who had
served only a few months, declined to ac
cept the order of dismissal, exhibiting a
written contract with the High School
Board to serve during one school year.
The Board of Education repudiated the
contract.

REPUBLICANS UNITED.
The Colon League of San Francisco has
informed the members of the Republican
campaign committee that It desires to
conduct one meeting under the auspices
of the club. There is talk of engaging the
Mechanics' Pavilion for one evening this
rnonth for the rally. Ten-minute speeches
will be the rule, and ten or twelve speak
er* representing workirgmen and business
men will deliver addresses.
The greeting given to Henry J. Crocker
attained the force and character of a
grf>at popular ovation. He was hailed by
thousands as the next Mayor of San
Francisco. The "hall was not half large
enough to accommodate all the citizens
that went to Jones and Eddy streets to
st-Â«Â» and hear Henry J. Crocker and his
ai-sociates on the ticket. Hundreds went
away, seeing that even standing room in
sidÂ«; was taken.
Th* magnitude of the Republican dem
onstration last Saturday night was the
leading topic of comment in the city yes
ttrday. The Democrats had a good meet
ing last Wednesday evening:, the audience
being a little larger than that which as
sembled early In the campaign to ratify
the nominations of the Union Labor
party. The Republican meeting, how
ever, in point of attendance and enthu
siasm outclassed all other political dem
orstrations cf the present campaign.
Referee Middleton repeatedly warned
the Spanish players against rough tactics
during the game, but evidently to no ac
count.
David Schloss, right tackle, was woe
fully mawled, and as a resu-- had his
collarbone fractured. Then in quick order
Frank Soule, right half, received a whiz
zing blow on the nose, and It was evident
at once that he was suffering much pain.
An examination showed that his nose had
been broken. Harry Stone, captain of the
team, also fell a victim to the rough play.
He suffered a broken leg, and In spite of
the pain he continued playing for several
seconds after he received the serious In
jury- The three maimed were taken to the
Presidio hospital, and later Schloss was
conveyed to the Park Emergency Hos
pital.
There was a game played at the Pre
sidio Athletic Grounds yesterday after
noon between the Arlington football club,
composed of members' of the First Bat
talion of Artillery. N. G. C, and La Union
club, which is made up of Spaniards w,ho
reside In San Francisco.
From the very beginning the Spaniards
Â¦were In a condition of physical and men
tal determination that the game should
go to them. With an enthusiasm that
hinged on ferocious excitement the game
was played with rough-and-tumble tactics
that would put in the shade those em
ployed at a bullfight.
There were only three men of the Ar
lington club laid low, and the only won
der is that there were not more. Their
injuries were such as to necessitate sur
gical aid, and for some weeks two at least
of the three will keep their beds under
medical care.
When Spanish and American football
devotees meet in friendly rivalry on the
field of sport it would be well, judging
from a game played on the outskirts of
the city yesterday, to have a corps of sur
geons and a half-dozen ambulances on the
spot.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Generally cloudy weather prevails over Wash
ington and fair weather in other districts west
of the Rocky Mountains. Light rain is reported
from Washington and Western Montana.
The, pressure has risen over the Pacific Slope
except over Southern Arizona and California,
where there *a* been a sharp fall.
The temperature has risen In the Sacramento
Valley and Southern Oregon and remained
nearly stationary in other districts.

hours ending midnight. October 12:

Special Dispatch to The Call
TACOMA, Wash., Oct. 11.â Four Klon
dike miners who- thawed sticks of dy
namite on a stove while cooking break
fast will not repeat their experiment very
soon. Elmer Crawford and three other
miners on Lovett Gulch tried this method
of saving time. The dynamite exploded,
badly injuring Crawford and slightly in
juring one other. The cabin was com
pletely wrecked and the men were hurled
out. One of Crawford's legs was lacer
ated In three places, twenty-five stitches
being necessary in sewing his wounds.
A dispatch from Dawson states that
the Upper Yukon steamer Mary Graff
was wrecked near Salmon lwiver early last
week. She was one of the largest steam
ers belonging to the White Pass Railway
and was hurrying down stream with a
full cargo from White Horse for Dawson.
She was beached to saVe her from sink
ing. It is doubtful if her cargo can be
landed at Dawson before navigation ends.
She carried no passengers.

G. Fitschen of Butte, Mont., one of San
Francisco's oldest marksmen, was on the
range, and spent the day in renewing old
acquaintanceships. . He took especial
pride In exhibiting the gold medal he won
at Alameda Schuetzen Park hi 1865 for
being the best shot in his company.
The event of the day was the fourth
annual eagle shooting of the Hannover
aner Verein. Much enthusiasm and ex
citement prevailed during the course of
the contest as piece after piece of the bird
was shot down. The excitement reached
the climax when Carsten Luhrs stepped
to the front and by a well-directed shot
brought down the last piece of the eagle,
thus making him the king of the festival.
He was immediately surrounded by his
comrades, who placed him in a chair,
mounted on a table, and carried him over
the grounds to the pavilion, where he was
crowned with the inslenia of his office
by Misses Henrietta Hillman and Annie
Vorwinkel. After a few well chosen
remarks. King Luhrs invited his subjects
to partake of refreshments.
The success of the festival was largely
due to the committee of arrangements,
composed of Herman Schult, president;
Chris ' Roeber. secretary; Frederick Eg
gers, Charles Kruse, Joseph Schllcht
rnann.
Shell Mound Park range was well filled
with riflemen yesteVday. The shooting
conditions were all that could bu expected,
consequently many good scores were
made. The Hannovaner Verein held its
annual king shooting and turned out the
full strength of the company. The Golden
Gate Rifle and Pistol Club, Deutscher
Kreiger Verein, San Francisco Schuetzen
Verein, Germania Schuetzen Club, Nord
deutscher Schuetzen Club and Independ
ent Rifles all held monthly contests.
A- man somewhat under . the Influence
of liquor walked up to Policeman F. H.
Flynn on Montgomery street yesterday
afternoon and said he was a deserter
from the army and as his conscience had
been"troubling him he wanted to give
himself up. He said his name was Arthur
H. Jones and he was a member of Com
pany C, Fifth Infantry, in 1899 and de
serted while the regiment was in Cuba.
He was locked up in. the City Prison and
the military authorities will be notified.
Says He Is a Deserter.

A large crowd of amateur sailors and
their friends witnessed the aquatic sports
given by the Sjn Francisco Yacht Club
yesterday at Sausalito. The day Waa
ideal, the air being warm, delightfully
clear and brilliant. The piazzas of the
clubhouse were filled with ladies in light
dresses and yachtsmen in natty nautical
costumes.
The first event, a swimming race for
niembers of the Olympic Club, was won
by H. Hinkel. A siniilar event for mem
bers of the San FTancisco Yacht Club
was won by R. W. Mason. Kirat prize
in the high divlnt contest was won by
J. Wise of the Olympic Club and second
prize by Giannini of the San Francisco
Yacht Club. The treble scullin? race in
the club barges was won by the crew of
the yawl Royal, made up of Dr. J. No
vltzky, bow; George Urquhart. waist;
Frank Maino. stroke, and W. L. Suther
land, coxswain. The swimming and dress
ing race excited a great deal of amuse
ment amons the spectators, each con
testant having to swim to three or four
yachts and don an article of attire on
each. R. W. Mason captured this event.
Several competitors essayed to walk tha
greasy pole, but all except R. W. Mason,
who appeared in feminine dress and was
disqualified for wearing stockings, failed
to reach the fiag at the end of the pole.
The double sculling race, in which
blindfolded competitors pulled according
to the directions of a rudderless lady cox
swain, was won by W. W. Haley and
his crew of the sloop Ariel. A crew of
the yacht Ariel also won the skiff race,
using the hands as paddles, and climbing
to the crosstrees of the yacht, where the
prize was hung.
A contest, in which a man armed with a
lonsr pole padded at the point and pro
pelled In a skiff by an oarsman, charged
another similarly equipped competitor,
endeavoring to push him from the boat
into the water, was won by Bradford of
the Olympic Club. Sydney Cavlll. the
swimming instructor of the Olympic Club,
gave an interesting and clever swimming
exhibition, in whjch he Imitated a seal,
a submarine boat, a lady swimmer, a
drowning man. etc. A race in Canadian
canoes was won by the two men in the
green craft, the couple In the red one
capsizing on rounding the stakeboat,
when they had a substantial lead.
The small yacht regatta,- for boats of
twenty feet and under, was won by the
yawl Witch, Angeline being second. The
sloops Alice, Zada and Mistral also com
peted. The course was from the bows of
the steamer Albatross round the revenue
cutter Thetis and the sloop Folly, an
chored off Hurricane Gulch, leaving the
marks on the port hand and finishing at
the point of starting. The wind was light
during the race.
Â» The officials of the day wore A. J.
Treat, starter: W. G. Morrow and C. M.
Gunn, judges; A. C. Lee, J..E. Gray, W.
W. Haley, R. S. Bridgman, W. G. Mor
row, Hillyer Deuprey and F. H. Much
more, entry solicitors. The prizes will be
presented at the closing entertainment of
the club on October 24.
The handicap singles tournament with
Its 104 entries was commenced on the park
tennis courts yesterday. The preliminary
round and part of the first round were
finished. The pleasant weather brought
out a large crowd of spectators, who were
treated to all kinds of matches. Some
were close and interesting, while the ma
jority were one-sided and devoid of skill.
Considering the large entry list the han
dicapping was well done. Most of the
poorer players were disposed of and the
matches from now on will be closer and
more interesting. None of the men, on
scratch or below lost except Grant Smith,
who defaulted.
R. N. Whitney, playing from "15 2-6"
back of scratch, had a hard time beat
ing Charles Dunlap. who was "2-Â«" behind
scratch. Whitney lost the first set, but
won the next two cleverly.
G. D. MacGavin. the crack California
Club player, showed his class when he
allowed Clarence Foley odds of "owe li"
and won In straight sets. Foley wa3
thought to have an excellent chance, bui
MacGavin won with little difficulty.
Clarence Grlffln and Norman Ambrose
played the closest and most exciting
match. Griffin allowed Ambrose "owe
2-6" and the handicap proved to be about
right. The third and deciding set ran
up to 9-7, and finally went to Griffln.
Horace Mann and Alonzo McFarlanct
also figured in a three-set match. All
three sets were 6-4 and two of them
went to Mann. Harry Rolfe won bom
his matches cleverly and reached the sec
ond round. He will meet Neil Baker oa
even terms and a good match will rÂ«
sult.

Lieutenant Foley Be turns.
Lieutenant Hamilton Foley of thÂ© Fifth
Infantry, who recently was dismissed
from the army, was among thÂ© passen
gers that arrived on the transport Sher
idan from Manila Saturday night. Ha
came home with his company under ar
rest. He was tried by court-martial in
the Philippines for conduct unbecoming
an officer and a gentleman and the sen
tence was dismissal and one year's Im
prisonment. The President remitted th*
imprisonment portion of the sentence.
BUCKINGHAM. Quebec. Oct. 11.â A land
slide occurred at Poupore. north of here, on
the Lievre River, to-day. The channel of the
river was completely blocked for a while and
many farm buildings were destroyed, but. so
far as reported, no lives were lost.

HANDBAXL EXPERTS PLAY
FAST EOTJB-HANDED GAME
Hampton and Levy and Bonnet and
Nealon BTeak Even After Six
Hot Games.
As usual the hard handball court of the
Olympic Club was crowded with specta
tors yesterday. The series of match
games which have been played for the
past two months brought many enthusi
asts to the clubhouse. James Fitzgerald,
the world's champion, was to have played
Jack Bonnet and Al Hampton yesterday,
but owing to a business engagement was
unable to show up. In order that the
spectators might not be disappointed a
four-handed game was made up between
Jack Bonnet and J. C. Ne&lon against
Al Hampton and Louis Levy. They play
ed six of the fastest games seen In the
club in months. Each side took three
games.
Bonnet's terrific toss and Nealon's
splendid playing- were features. Hampton
suffered a bad shoulder and did not play
up to form. However, he was in every
play and x Â»dld great work. Levy's show
ing was very good. He has not been
playing as long as the others, but Is rap
idly becoming an expert. The scores
were 21-16. 21-12. 21-15, &-21. 18-21, 2-21. Nea
lon played Levy a single-handed game
and beat him by 8 points. Although an
aged man Nealon plays a remarkable
game. He drives the ball with great
force and commands it nicely. Fitzgerald
and Bonnet and Hampton will play next
Sunday. The soft handball tournament
will open at the Olympic Club to-night.

At the quarterly meeting of the St. Pat
rick's Mutual Alliance Association, which
was held last Saturday evening, the re
ports of the officers showed that the so
ciety has within the last three months
raised its. general fund to 110.000, an in
crease of a little over 3 per cent. Secre
tary Alford reviewed the work accom
plished by the society in the last three
months, and the committee on arrange
ments reported that the necessary prep
arations for the celebration of the St.
Patrick's Mutual Alliance Association's
thirtieth anniversary had been made, and
will -take place at Union Square Hall on
Friday evening, October 30.

Fountain Pen News.
Dr. Alexander J. Mclvor-Tyndall enter
tained and mystified a large audience at
Steinway Hall last night with demonstra
tions in occult phenomena. It was one or
the most brilliant gatherings of the sea
son, and the famous psychic scientist was
In excellent trim. - He delivered an excel
lent discourse on the destructive power of
thought, citing many, cases of personal
experience: where thought vibrations of a
certain potentiality have been instru
mental in producing chemical changes in
the human organism that have . resulted
in death, or, as he calls it, disintegration.
Dr. .Molvor-Tyndall ia a' bold and original
thinker, and while he Is probably manv
years in advance of the age with his pe
culiar views of life, yet he adheres to the
latest developments of material science in
expressing his novel theories of the psy
chological side of life. The lecture was
evidently much appreciated and was re
ceived with enthusiasm.
Dr. Tyndall's Lecture.
It may be noted in passing that the manipulators
of these unnatural combinations have had an excel
lent excuse, from their standpoint, for thejr attacks
on President Roosevelt and their accusation that he
is the cause of the tumbling of their card house.
With a very clear prevision he saw, the end from the
beginning. He saw that ' these over-capitalized
schemes were destined to collapse when the people
ceased to buy inflated stocks. Therefore he insisted
upon a degree of publicity that would warn the people
against such investment. His course made it impos
sible for the underwriting syndicate to sell stocks at
thirty times their actual value and so caused the col
lapse of the shipbuilding trust and the present down
fall of every watered combination. He has rendered
a public service as great as that of Mr. Cleveland
when he sustained the public credit against exactly
such a proposition in linance. His fame has outlived
the denunciation of the mefn who wanted to water all
the money of the country by falling to a silver basis
and making the people take 45 cents for 100 cents. ."
President Roosevelt started the machinery of the
law in motion, and though the trust organs have at-
THE disclosures incident to the examination of
the affairs of the shipbuilding trust h, av e prob
ably struck a death blow to future combinations
of capital by means having no other motive than
enrichment of the promoters. It seems quite sure
that the power of J. Pierpont Morgan is broken and
that no other will succeed him as the wizard of com
bination. We long ago predicted that the time would
come when every over-capitalized enterprise would
get the water squeezed out of it and the people would
be taught once for all that there was a^atural limit
to combination beyond which no artifice, no matter
how cunning and expert, could sustain business in
violation of the natural laws of commerce.
It is well that this time has come now. The coun
try can stand the consequences. There is a slowing
up of trade and cautionary signals are abundant, but
legitimate operations will sustain the shock. There
is no use trying to conceal the fact that it is the most
serious, in all our industrial and financial history. It
is great in proportion to the wide departure from cor
rect principles which has caused it. Ten years ago
it would have convulsed the commerce of the coun
try from the top down to the smallest peanut stand,
for we were largely in debt abroad and our public
finances were disordered at home. Now the foreign
balance against us is imperceptibly small and our
finances arc on a far sounder basis.
THE TRUSTS TUMBLING.
Under the benign influence of American institu
tions, encouraged and enforced at the point of the
bayonet, the Moros have decided that they will have
no more slave hunting in their provinces. This con
cession to our civilization is encouraging. It may
not be too much to expect that when the Moros
fight with us, in consequence of periodical disagree
ments, they will decide to restrict their operations
to what we are pleased to call civilized warfare.
The country's bank clearings are still running be
hind those of 1002, the loss last week being 137 per
cent, though the aggregate clearings have increased
sharply during the past ten days from the low point
of Si ,400,000,000 to the good showing of $2,180,000,000.
The loss in percentage is not uniform, about half of
the largest cities exhibiting gains over 1902. Fail
ures last week were 223, against 245 for the same week
last 3'ear. Our foreign trade diminished last week,
both imports and exports falling off, though the loss
in both was not^eaw.
Conditions in Wall street have not been satisfactory
of late, as the sinister developments in connection
with the internal affairs of the United States Steel and
Shipbuilding trusts have still further shaken 'public
confidence in the stock market The great trusts
seem to, have reached that point predicted several
years ago by Henry Clews, the astute and
far-seeing New York banker, who even then saw
what was coming and said that the public need not
worry about the huge trusts â they would settle them
selves if given rope enough and left alone.
The iron and steel trade furnishes the most notable
example of this increase in production and decrease
in demand. The United States Steel Corporation re
ports that its orders now are 1,000,000 tons less than
a year ago, whereas on the 1st of January they were
1,000.000 tons larger than at the same time the year
before, while all the leading producers of pig iron
have agreed to cut down the output. The New Eng
land cotton mills are running on reduced time and
some are storing their products. The footwear fac
tories of the same section report operations fair,
though prices are less satisfactory and the margin of
profit is smaller. The cotton crop is not turning out
as well as anticipated and the movement at New
Orleans is being deranged by a labor strike.
Provisions continue to <frag at Chicago and other
Western centers and the market is being held up, as
private reports from Chicago express it, "by sheer
muscle." The tendency in farm staples is now down
ward rather than upward and cereals on the Chicago
Board of Trade are weak. There is one good feature
to this, which is that if the American wheat' market
keeps down to the European export basis we shall be
in a position to sell wheat to Europe, whereas for
months we were handicapped in this business by our
own high prices. The same condition applies to pig
iron. Increased production and lower prices will
rheck our importations of this product from abroad
and may increase our local business. So lower grain
md iron markets are not altogether an unmixed evil.
Dry goods are reported quieter at New York and
Texas reports some falling off in business there and
in other parts of the Southwest, largely owing to the
Dackv.-ardness of the cotton crop. But no trouble in
rollections from this cause has yet been reported.
The continuation of labor troubles, though by no
means as acute as some months ago, is diminishing
building operations, reducing the demand for
structural material and making the lumber market
The best reports are still from the western half of
the country and the very best are from the Pacific
slope, where the volume of business is keeping up
excellently. The railroad earnings still lead those of
last year by about 8 per cent, and while the com
plaints of car shortage are by no means as numerous
or loud as for several years past, there is still a de
ficiency in cars to move the goods tendered the rail
roads.
TRADE conditions are undergoing a decided
change. Everybody now sees it. Close com
mercial observers have seen it coming for
months. But the change is in the nature of a sur
prise to even the latter and is but another illustration
of the old saying that .we never know how things are
going to turn out. In the first place the money mar
ket, which everybody expected would be stringent at
this time when the crops are beginning to move, is
not particularly so, and in fart is easier than it was
some months ago. In the ft-cond place there is a
pronounced downward tendeircy in prices for almo-t
everything, accompanied by larger supplies of almost
all products. In other words production has at last
overtaken consumption and in many lines is now
passing it. This means lower prices for these prod
uct* and indicates that the cost of living from now
on will decrease rather than increase. It was not
expected that this increase in supplies and recession
in prices would occur before next spring, but it is
even now with us.

ALL' PABTIES SATISFIED.
There is not the slightest possibility of a
row in our church about ritualism, either in
New York . or elsewhere. The Bishops recog
nize thoroughly that incense, lights, vestments
and all the ceremonies of the mass are strictly
legal in the American church. The prejudice
about ritual, due to historical reasons in con
nection with the Church of Rome, is rapidly
dying out. Slowly but surely Catholic cere
monial Is being adopted In all of our churches.
There is no attempt made to oppose the Cath
olic movement. Moreover, the old errors Identi
fied with the names "low church" and "broad
church" are gradually passing away. If there
are any "parties" --in our church, and a church
to be alive must have "parties," there is, at
least, with very few exceptions, a mutual cour
tesy and charity which unites them in a bond
stronger than their differences.
Dr. Greer, however, is rather a practical
man of affairs than a scholar or theologian.
He is regarded as a man of wide reading In
general lines, and he is a striking preacher.
His popularity is the more remarkable in that
Dr. Qreer Is peculiar and absolutely frigid in
manner. He has a waxed mustache, parts his
hair in the middle and dresses like an affected
floorwalker of a fashionable dry-goods store â
which would impress one atjfnrst somewhat
unfavorably. Respect for hijh, however, grows
with acquaintance. He is honest, fearless, able
and kind.
indissolublllty of marriage. He has expressed
himself publicly since on the same subject with
even greater force. There . was a fear at one
time that "low" or "broad" views on the
subject of divorce would make some way In the
church, especially in fashionable New York.
The election of Dr. Greer Is an indication that
the diocese of New York will not favor laxity
on the divorce question.

The school near San Luis Obispo is the first of its
kind in respect to location.- Heretofore such institu
tions have been placed in the large cities. This one
is practically in the country, in > the midst of whole
some surroundings, where those who seek to enjoy
its opportunities will not be diverted by the allure
ments of a great city. To it the country youth may
be expected to resort. Successful ranch manage
ment in California requires such a school. The
owner or the foreman of a ranch should know how to
shot a horse, to plan and build, to take machinery
down and set it up. In the near future electric
power is to be extensively used in running Califor
nia ranches. It will operate pumps, distribute water,
run stationary, machines for threshing grain and
beans, and will do the churning in creameries. In
these schools boys will learn something of elec
trical engineering and the mechanism to be actuated
by electricity, so that this new labor-aiding agent
may be handled by them.
To return to conditions in this, city, for the pur
pose of emphasis. "We have here, operated in ; con
junction, the Lick and Wilmerding manual schools,
established by private endowment. % The Lick School
has-415 pupils, and for lack of facilities is compelled
to turn away more than that number who apply.
Instead of establishing the semi-penal departments
proposed by the School Superintendent, why. not de
vote what they would cost to extension of that
polytechnic school, so that its capacity would equal
the demands upon it?
If more study is given to leading the young in
line with these natural tendencies, and less to the use
of force to compel them into things that are dis
tasteful, we will be training better citizens: and if
as much attention is paid to this as is given to the
penal plan, the happy time may come when the
State schools. Â£or incorrigible youth may be turned
into polytechnic institutions, to which pupils resort
voluntarily and cheerfully for training in handicrafts
and skilled occupations. . ' ,
The policy that creates these conditions is a mis
taken one. Not every boy who goes to a trade at
the proper age makes a finished mechanic. Only
about one-tenth of all the apprentices have the qual
ity that induces them to always remain in the use
and practice of a trade. The highest purpose of
their training in the trade is to teach them industry,,
thrift, temperance and economy, the training that
makes for good citizenship, and they apply these
qualities in lines of life suited to their tastes, often
iar away from the trade in which they were acquired.
It is therefore an error to presume that apprenticeship
should be denied or so limited that the privilege is
useless, in order to malce trained men in the trades
scarce and thereby make the control of labor easier
and give it the features of a monopoly, enjoyed
mostly by aliens.
The denial or limitation of apprenticeship has
made the polytechnic school necessary, and it is well
that such institutions are increasing in number,' for
they have become the bulwark of good citizenship.
Where they are made part of the public school sys
tem of large cities the most interesting effects have
been observed. Before their establishment a per
centage of pupils, by indifference to the study of
books and a tendency to truancy, reduced the stand
ing" of all grades above the primary. Where the trade
schools are established it is observed that this class
of indifferent and truant pupils, almost without ex
ception, voluntarily enroll themselves in the "shop
school," where they at once become interested and
indutlrious workers. It will be observed that this
improves the morale of the whole School Depart
ment, reduces truancy to a minimum, raises the stand
ing of the grades affected, and is the means of
checking and effacing the tendency to idleness and
indifference.
The City Superintendent of San Francisco has re
cently proposed the establishment of truant and
parentaLschools, in which the class of pupils referred
to are to be separated and kept under conditions that,
after all. are semi-penal. In the training of the young
it is not well to classify thejp according to their faults
and shortcomings, as would be* the case in such
schools as the Superintendent proposes. It is, on the ]
other hand, far better to cause them to voluntarily
seek congenial occupation. No natural trait is more
universal than the fondness of the young for
mechanism and their desire to make something with
their hands. The boy loves his pocket-knife, and
with it whittles something into shape. It was said
that the whittling habit of the Yankee was the foun
dation of the early manufacturing supremacy of New
England, and so it was written among the classics
of the old and excellent school readers:
The Yankee boy, before he's sent to school,
Knows how to use that magic tool,
The pocket knife.
THE opening of the. new State polytechnic
school, near San Luis Obispo, is an event of
peculiar interest. ' The State can better afford
to endow such institutions than to build reform
schools for the reception of youthful incorrigibles,
under semi-penal conditions. The young need oc
cupation. They need the ordinary opportunities of
fered by the public schools as a preliminary, but
while yet in their teens they need the training of
mind and hand in some manual industry.
The restrictions upon apprenticeship and the al
most entire disappearance of the "open shop" have
had a disastrous effect '.upon our native-born yputh.
That effect is seen in the steady increase of â¢ the
semi-penal institutions in which large numbers are
restrained. While they are thus branded the. places
they should occupy under normal conditions are
filled by foreign immigration, and the country is
sacrificing the benefit it would get from the upright*
citizenship of those born on its soil and reared to
know the genius of its institutions and respect its
laws.
ANOTHER TRADE SCHOOL
As a Cabinet maker Premier Balfour is anything
but a glittering success. Even his well wishers, who
know of no Ministerial crisis as serious as the pres
ent since the last of Gladstone, are sorrowfully of
the opinion that Mr. Balfour should try his hand at
another trade. Cabinet-making is too intricate for
potterers.

The contract of the Philippine Govern
ment with the Uraga Dock Company in
Japan for the construction of five gun
boats has turned out badly. Only two of
the boats have been delivered, and the\'
failed to come up to the requirements, as
they drew more water than intended, and
their construction and workmanship were
generally defective. The Uraga builders
have be^n notified that the other three
boats will not be accepted, and that the
guarantee check for the two boats deliv
ered is forfeited. Several payments hava
already been made upon the three con
tracts now suspended, and which the
'company refuses to refund, but is said
to have made concessions satisfactory to
the Government. The Philippine Govern
ment has wasted a lot of money and
time on this transaction, which is not a Â¦
surprise to builders on this coast. The
boats could have bÂ«en built here as waa i
advocated early last year, and if too^
small for safe navigation could have been
shipped in sections to Manila for comple
tion.

A unique warship has been added as an
auxiliary to the Swedish navy. It Is
built like an iron lighter. Is about 130
feet In length, and will serve as a trans
port, carrying a military balloon* and ita
accessories, and contains the necessary
machinery for producing hydrogen and
maneuvering the balloon. The lighter is
towed by a steamer, which conveys it
from point to point wherever it may be
desired to make an ascension. The crew
consists of two officers and sixteen sail
ors. The balloon itself Is to be used for
obtaining information along the coast in
defense of the latter, and will prove a
valuable adjunct to the usual service of
security and information.

seven cruisers, eight gunboats, one tor

The British armored cruiser Cumber
land. 9800 tens, built by the London and
Glasgow Company, has passed through
her trials without a hitch and consider
ably exceeded the contract requirement.
The eight-hour trial under full power
took place September 2. The ship was
down to a mean draught of 2V-" 2 feet and
fully equipped. With steam at 296 pounds
In her Belleville boilers the engines de
veloped 22,767 horsepower, or 767 horse
power over the contract, giving a speel
of 23.7 knots, an excess of .7 knots over
the calculated speed. The coal consump
tion was 2.01 pounds per horsepower.

The several classes of vessels authorized

The personnel in the British is now
more than double of what it was fifteen
years ago. In 1SSS the number was 62,400,
now It is 127,100. The highest number in
the past was 146,312 In 1810, during the
Napoleonic war. The highest figures in
the navy estimates during that war was
in 1814, when they amounted to niT,500,
000, an enormous sum for a country of
18,000,000 people. The cost of the navy
was then 16 per cent of the value of the
British sea-borne trade, and at the pres
ent time, with navy estimates of $172,
000.000, it 'is only 2 per cent of that trade.
It is true Mayor Schmitz can no longer pretend,
save by effrontery, to be the representative or even
the friend of union labor, nor indeed the friend or rep
resentative of anything beyond the Ruef machine
and the discontented masses that are willing to accept
it. His record since his election has been that of a
lightning change amateur. Hardly had he learneJ of
his election when he wrote effusive thanks, not to the
leaders of the Labor party and to the workingmen
who had been deluded into supporting him, but to
Abe Rucf and to such malcontent Republicans as
had followed Ruef. A little later he declared himself
a Republican and then almost immediately hastened
away to New York to support a Democratic candi
date for Congress.
As the intelligence of the workingmen of the city
may naturally be expected to guard them against
being deceived into supporting Schmitz and Ruef a
second time it may be assumed in some quarters that
the machine is no longer dangerous. Such assump
tions, however, must not be* blindly trusted. There
is blazing and blaring evidence all over town that
Schmitz and Ruef have money to burn in electric
lights arSi to blow to the winds through trombones
and trumpets. An element in the city that is power
ful enough to furnish so much in the way of a cam
paign fund for a discredited administration and a pair
of jack-o'-lantern politicians is an element powerful
enough to be dangerous.
A union of the conservative elements of the city is
therefore imperative, and that union to be effective
must be organized behind Henry J. Crocker. The
Democratic party is hopelessly divided. Any one
who watches the campaign can see a hundred evi
dences almost daily that the Hearst wing of the
party is going to knife Lane, partly to get him out of
politics and partly to make a showing that will seem
ingly justify their assertion that he is looked upon as
a tool of McNab, "the avowed enemy of labor," as
the Examiner styled him. The only possible way
then to defeat the Schmitz-Ruef or the "Ritz-
Schmuef combintfion is to vote for Henry J.
Crocker. That is the plain issue. Now let the people
go to it.
BY the developments of the campaigning for the
week that closed on Saturday night the domi
nant issue of the time was brought plainly
into public view. The ratification meetings of the
three parties with the speeches of the candidates for
Mayor and for other important offices outlined the
programme of each party, while the character of the
audiences assembled at the different gatherings
served to reveal the nature of the forces that are back
of each. Other events, meetings and speeches of the
week tended to further clear up the situation and re
move every confusing elejnent. We begin the second
week of the campaign therefore with an accurate con
ception of the situation. Every intelligent voter now
knows that the task imposed upon our citizenship at
this juncture is that of saving San Francisco from
the menace of class politics and from incompetent and
perhaps corrupt administration.
The lesson of the last municipal election remains
fresh in the memory of conservative men of both the
great parties and of all classes of the people. If the
conservative elements be divided we shall again see
a triumph of a faction that is ostensibly working in
the interests of a single class, but whose record in of
fice proves it to be working for^the selfish purposes
of a few individuals who are really as regardless of the
interests of the workingmen whose votes they ob
tained as they pretend to be of the interests of the
corporations they denounced. If that sort of politics is
to be rebuked, if that kind of administration is to be
discarded, there must be on the part of all citizens who
are opposed to the Schmitz-Ruef machine a har
monious movement toward the election of Mr.
Crocker and of his colleagues on the Republican
ticket.

Whatever hopes remained cf the- prospect for
saving the British ship Glfford were sent to
smash yesterday by the waves, which battered
the rand trapped derelict. At sundown only
part of the foremast was standing:. The other
masts one by one succumbed to the Jar of the
ocean's bombardment, and as they fell made
havoc of the ship's stout hull. The decks
were awash all of yesterday, the bulwarks have
disappeared and what Is left of the Gifford is
only an Iron shell, which every hour Is becom
ing more firmly Imbedded In the treacherous
tand.

Gaston Berkaut, a cook's helper, 16 years
of age, had a fierce battle with the waves
at the ocean beach yesterday. He was
sitting on the shore when he was picked
up by a large breaker and carried ocean
ward.- For some time he was tossed about
in the water and had it not been for the
assistance rendered by several, spectators
the lad would have lost his life.
Young Berkaut has not been in the coun
try very long. Since his arrival he has
been employed in a Bush-street restau
rant. He lost his job yesterday morning
and went out to the beach to think over
his ill fortune. After rambling up and
down the shore for some time he became
weary and sat down on a small rock that
protruded from the sand close to the wa
ter's edge. For a time all went well, the
splash reaching his feet at times, but he
seemed beyond the reach of danger.
Suddenly a giant wave approached and
Berkaut did not realize his peril until it
was upon him. He had no time to escape,
for in an instant he was submerged and
when the water receded he was swept out
with it. He struggled bravely against
drowning. .He was tossed about, but the
strength of the breakers lifted him from
his feet each time he sought to gain a
place, of safely. His cries attracted the
attention of several people who were in
the neighborhood and two strangers went
to his assistance.

ISLAND PORTS.
ple of King Solomon's Ludge No.
MO. F. and A. M-, at Its new building
s!te, Fillmcre etreet, near gutter.
This ledge held its first meeting Janu
ary Â£7, iSSO, at Hamilton Hall, with twen
ty-two members, A. F. Knorp being its
first worshipful master. Of these mem
bers very few are now alive, all of the
officers having passed away. William
Parker Filmer is the present worshipful
master. King Solomon's Lodge has al
ways stood in the first rank in Masonic
THE most important event in Ma
sonic circles In San Francisco for
some time will be the laying to
day of the cornerstone of the tem-
At 11 o'clock this morning the members
of King Solomon's Lodge and visiting
brethren will assemble and with the
Grand Lodge, California Commandery,
ThÂ£ new temple will be a handsome
structure, designed of brick and etone,
five stories In height, occupying the en?
tire lot, 50x100 feet. The basement is fit
ted for a banquet hall. The second floor
will be used for a lodge hall. Th6 upper
floor will be -devoted to King Solomon's
Lodge and other Masonic lodges and bod
ies, with parlors, ante-rooms, organ loft
and every necessary convenience for
these purposes. The rear portion of the
building will be fitted up as a social hall
for the use of Masonic bodies.
The idea of building a temple in the
Western Addition was started some years
ago. A more central or better location
could not be found than Fillmore street,
between Sutler and Post. The building
is being erected under the supervision of
Architect Fred B. Wood, a member of the
lodge, who has designed as handsome a
structure as will be- found in the Western
Addition.
circles and has now a membership of
more than 400.
Every Masonic 16dge in the jurisdiction,
all worshipful .masters and wardens and
the most prominent Masons have been in
vited Â«to attend this celebration.
will proceed In procession under direction
of Major George Kilmer,' marshal, to the
site of the building, where the worship
ful master, William P. Filmer, after mak
ing; the opening address, will present to
the most worshipful grand master, Orrin
S. Henderson, a suitably inscribed trowel.
Following that will be the usual Ma
sonic ceremonies incident to the laying
of a cornerstone, interspersed . with or
gan and vocal music.
It is the Intention of King Solomon's
Lodge to make this an auspicious occa
sion. The officers of the lodge are: Wil
liam Parker Filmer, worshipful master;
George W. Lake, senior warden; Clarence
T. Cleve, junior warden; Harry Baehr,
secretary: Benjamin F. Jellison, treas
urer: Samuel B. Stevenson, senior dea
con; George W. Davis, junior deacon;
Ira S. Hobbs, marshal; Fred A. Earle,
senior steward; Edward A. Janssen, Jun
ior steward; A. A. Batkin, organist;
George C. Dawson. tyler; William P. Fil
mer, B. F. Jellison, Harry Baehr, trus
tees. . . â¢ - '''".-.

ThÂ© prosy old professor Is a scientific grent, â¢
His talk is often so abstruse his friend don't know what's meant.
If you're writing cut an ad you mustn't he like him.
But make it plain as wcrds can be and full of snap and vim.
And then besides you must be sure to put It in The Call, .
For you will nnd as others have it pays the best of all.Not wishing to take any chances, the
company decided to build the road on
Sunday, on which day an injunction could
not be secured or served. About 300 men
were employed all day in tearing up the
street, and to-night a force of over 100
men is at work. All day long Market
street has been thronged, and the great
est enthusiasm has been shown. The sen
timent of the entire city is with the build
ers of the San Jose-Los Gatos railway.
To-day's railroad building is believed to
mark an era in a greater San Jose. The
work of construction was taken up near
the City Hall Park, where work was
stopped a week ago. The pavement was
removed, ties laid on the street founda
tion and rails strung as far as Santa
Clara street before midnight to-night. A
move will probably be made by the pro
testants in court to-morrow.
On Friday the City Store, a corporation,
brought suit in tbe Superior Court to se
cure a permanent injunction against the
railway. It was alleged that the building
of the road would be detrimental to its
property. It was understood that as soon
as the work of construction was com
menced the City Store would apply for a
temporary Injunction and stop the work.
SAN JOSE, Oct. 1Lâ The San Jose-Los
Gatos Inter urban Electric Railway stole
a march on the protesting property own
ers along Market street, between San Fer
nando and Santa Clara, early this morn
ing.
About 6 o'clock Contractor G. W. Elder
and a force of 300 men appeared on the
scene and began tearing up Market street
for the roadbed of the track. This stretch
of track was needed to give the line a city
end. Never before had railroad building
been carried on in the Garden City on a
Sunday. About 300 people cheered the
railroad builders.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
San Jose Street Torn Up
on Sunday to Avoid
Courts.
RAILROAD COUP
AMUSES CITIZENS
CHICAGO, Oct. 11.â Charles A. Good
now will sever his connection -with the
Chicago, 'Rock Island and Pacific Railroad
as general manager at the end of the
present month. Though his resignation
was forecasted several weeks ago. It was
not formally presented until yesterday.
Fourth Vice President John F._ Stevens,
who recently came to the Rock Island
from the Great- Northern, will have
charge oC^the operations and construction,
and it has been semi-offlclally announced
that B. " F. "Winchell, now vice president
and general manager of the St. Louis and
San Francisco, Is to be third vice presi
dent of the Rock Island and will be given
supreme charge of the traffic affairs of
all the lines of the system. Winchell It Is
said will move from St. Louis to Chicago
on November 1. It is also stated that
Goodnow*s position as general manager
will not be filled after tils retirement. '
General Manager of the Railroad Pre
sents His Resignation to the
Board of Directors.

Popular Excursions
To the East.
There are many reasons why our
Personally Conducted excursion parties -
are so popularâ one of them is the char-
acter of our special conductor service.
Our conductors are picked men.
They must be competent and well
informed, of course, but besides that
their manner and way of taking care '
of things must be acceptable to our
patrons.
A cheery, good-natured, willing, well-'
informed conductor can add greatly to
the comfort and pleasure of the party.
Our conductors are of ' this sort.
You can rely upon that.
Kindly inquire about our Personally
Conducted parties before you make up
your mind how to go East.

Chicago or St. Louis.

SAUSAL.ITO. Oct. 11.â The city of Sau

In Kiatinc. China, a man was recently
executed by - being nailed to a wooden

cumbed to his tortures.
A young man who registered at the
Grand Hotel on Saturday night as Wil
liam Jordan, South San Francisco, was
found dead in bed yesterday afternoon
with the gas turned fully on. The door,
window and transom were tightly closed.
He had been dead for some hours. The
Morgue was notified and Dej?uty Coroner
Fennell removed the body.
It was evidently a caae of deliberate
suicide. It was shortly .before midnight
that the young man entered the office of
the hotel and said he wanted a bedroom
for the night. He was assigned to one on
the top floor. He did not even have a
satchel with him and his clothing was
not by any means - costly. His manner
gave no indication that he contemplated
ending his life. -
About 1 o'clock yesterday afternon Kit
ty Cregan, a chambermaid, detected the
odor of gas coming from the young man's
room and she informed J. Ollphant, a
porter, of the fact. Ollphant forced open
the door of the bedroom -and discovered
the dead body. The room was full of gas.
When Deputy Coroner, Fennell searched
the clothing of the young man nothing
was found that would* give a clew to hi*
identity. There were two plugs of tobac
co, an election card and - an ' empty
purse. There -was not a cent of money In
the pockets, which, may. have been the
cause for the rash act. .
' ThÂ© deceased was about 27 years of age,
five feet seven inches tall and of slim
build. He had gray eyes, fine teeth, a
prominent nose and long blonde hair. His
clothing consisted of a brown sack coat
and vest, light striped trousers, tweed
cap, blue sweater with red stripes, blue
underclothing and black lace shoes.
Young Man Found Dead
in Bed With Gas
Turned On.
